B007704HO6|5.0|A Street Cat Named Bob by new author James Bowen is the most heart-warming book I've read since Vickie Myron's Dewey: The Small-Town Library Cat Who Touched The World. Bowen's story of his misadventures, mistakes, culminating with his falling through the cracks of modern society and ultimately his rescue by new-found friend Bob is the epitome of the modern fairy tale. Instead of the knight in shining armor saving the day, salvation comes from the most humble and unexpected place. Bob is a Frodo for animal lovers everywhere.<br /><br />  Without giving away the entire book, James is a man who grew up in England and in Australia. He had a rough childhood because of moving so much and this created in him many difficulties in socializing normally. This drove him ultimately away from family and into drugs and, as is often the case in these situations, the streets. Along the way he made a few attempts to save himself but never could. That is until a cat named Bob showed up on his doorstep. This new friendship proved to be the spark that got him back on his feet ultimately.<br /><br />  The anecdotes that Bowen gives make me reminisce about my own cats through the years as well the general adventures that all pet owners experience when they have a good relationship with their quadruped friends. This journey not only documents a beautiful and epitomizing modern-day fairy tale come-true but also strays in a political direction. I felt it shows how social programs that provide a hand-up are better than those that just give a hand-out. That is my thought, not necessarily the author's. All around, this was one very heartwarming adventure.<br /><br />  I strongly recommend this book and give a five star rating. The Kindle edition which I read seemed almost entirely typo-free with but one exception that I noticed. The book reads fast and should appeal to all cat lovers, most pet lovers and anyone who is a fan of the triumph of friendship and the human err... feline spirit. If you buy no other book this month, buy this one!buy no other book this month, buy this one!

B001E3AXGS|5.0|As the author of Lincoln, Inc.: Selling the Sixteenth President in Contemporary America, I've read at least four-score and seven Lincoln biographies out of the roughly 18,000 books written on the Great Emancipator. But Doris Kearns Goodwin's Team of Rivals ranks in my top five. There are some other biographies that offer more comprehensive details on Lincoln's life, and there are some that offer more complex analyses of his childhood, his political philosophy, his rhetoric, his moral compass, and his personal relationships. But to my mind, no other biography does a better job of bringing the context of Lincoln's presidency to life. The book offers intimate and memorable portraits of Lincoln's friends and \\"rivals,\\" helping the reader understand the particular traits that set Lincoln apart. The book is not without its flaws and limitations (which have been addressed by many professional historians), but in my opinion it is essential reading for anyone interested it better understanding Lincoln and his age.

I maintain it is silly to demand someone read half a series to understand the first book.<br /><br />I fear too many people mistake density and difficulty for creativity and innovation. No. Innovation was Joyce with \\"Ulysses\\". Creativity was Tolkein. This was just a waste of my time. Fortunately, I borrowed it, so I am not out a penny. God bless our public library!

B003WUYOQ6|5.0|Taubes wrote this book as a follow-on to his previous book, hoping this one would be more \\"user friendly.\\" And it is.<br /><br />The subject of dieting is a complex one. There is a lot at stake. First and foremost, one's health. But there is a lot of money to be made in the whole diet industry and so, like many other things in this country, it becomes very difficult to get the big picture or to know what is true or not. Magazine ads, TV ads, multitudes of \\"products\\" bearing multitudes of claims...all aimed at the American people, many of whom are getting fatter and fatter, many of whom have developed diabetes, or whose health is even further compromised by obesity in a myriad of ways. A medical industry which has lost its way, swallowed up by the pharmaceutical industry. The agriculture industry which has a stake in what people eat, and the easier it is to grow and get to market, the more money they can make. The food industry itself which has super-sized everything, which has promoted a dizzying array of processed food meant to save the busy American some time. Food that is processed for shelf life, food that should mold after a few days, so loaded with chemicals that it is still sitting on the counter weeks to months later...is this what we should be putting in our bodies?<br /><br />So we are fat. As a nation, as a society. We have been fed so much information for so many decades now, that it is impossible to understand what to do. How to do something real and useful to bring back our health, to help us lose weight.<br /><br />Why We Get Fat is a big book. There are a lot of pages to read. When your life and your health is at stake, the information is riveting. It gives you a perspective like none other. I read it in one night. My husband read it in one day. We came away with an accurate perception of the diet research over the 20th century, and where the research and advice veered away from a healthy approach to what we have all been sold for the last 50 years,namely to eat low-fat and high carbohydrate...It is clear that in the last fifty years something has gone very wrong with nutrition in the US. How else would the staggering increases in obesity and diabetes have occurred?<br /><br />Taubes does a good job explaining the physiological and hormonal  basis for metabolic syndrome/insulin resistance...the underlying reasons for the explosion of Type 2 Diabetes, even in children. And he does a good job of saying what a person can do to reverse this process.<br /><br />Keep in mind, though it is not in this book, that there is new thought out there that is saying that there may be a reason to call Alzheimers Disease \\"Type 3 Diabetes.\\" It would make sense. How else could there be such an explosion of Alzheimers across such a broad swath of our country.<br /><br />Taubes does a great job of pointing out the reasons for the fattening of America. A good job of pointing out a way to reverse the process.<br /><br />Since following the recommendations in this book, I have lost 30 pounds, and feel so much better. I no longer crave sweets, or starchy carbohydrates. I have good, steady energy. I sleep much better. For the first time in five decades of yo-yo dieting I understand how this way of eating will be a delicious way to live out my life. We essentially eat a Paleo diet, we do not eat processed food at all, we spend a bit extra for pasture raised meat, for wild-caught fish, and we get our veggies at an organic farm...we have decided that \\"food is medicine.\\" And the bonus is, real food doesn't take much time to prepare, is actually very delicious with out all the sauces and processed ingredients. I would rather spend my money on healthy food now, than a lot of medicine later.<br /><br />Bravo Gary Taubes for tackling this subject and taking on the diet and food industry in the process.

B005GSZZ1A|5.0|Americans' sources of knowledge about life in North Korea are limited to journalistic accounts of the diplomatic antics of the ruling Kim dynasty, which reveal practically nothing, and a tiny number of credible and highly readable books written by Westerners with rare access to the facts. In recent years, two stand out: Nothing to Envy, by Barbara Demick, and, amazingly, a novel, The Orphan Master's Son, by Adam Johnson. With Escape from Camp 14, veteran reporter Blaine Harden has contributed the latest of these reports, and in many ways the most revealing.<br /><br />Escape from Camp 14 chronicles the life of Shin Dong-hyuk, only of only three people ever known to have escaped from a North Korean labor camp and made it to the West. Shin was born in the camp, his mother \\"assigned\\" to his father by guards to bear children. Both were prisoners. A superficial reading of Shin's story might suggest that he is a monster. Reading the book will help you understand how he could commit unforgivable acts as natural and necessary steps in a ferocious day-to-day struggle for survival. \\"His context had been twenty-three years in an open-air cage run by men who hanged his mother, shot his brother, crippled his father, murdered pregnant women, beat children to death, taught him to betray his family, and tortured him over a fire.\\"<br /><br />As one of Harden's North Korean expatriate sources explained, \\"`guards were free to indulge their appetites and eccectricities, often preying on attractive young women prisoners, who would usually consent to sex for better treatment. `If this resulted in babies, women and their babies were killed,' [the source] said, noting that he had personally seen newborns clubbed to death with iron rods.\\"<br /><br />When Shin fled Camp 14, it was not a vision of freedom that gave him the extraordinary courage required. It was thoughts of grilled meat. To understand this extraordinary statement, you'll have to read the book.<br /><br />Blaine Harden appeared recently in San Francisco at the World Affairs Council of Northern California in a conversation with Philip Yun, Executive Director of the Ploughshares Fund, a former U.S. diplomat who is Korean-American and has studied the peninsula for many years. Harden's soft-spoken, sometimes light-hearted presentation underlined the surreal quality of Shin's experiences inside the North Korean gulag. Responding to Yun's questions, Harden spoke at some length about the many steps he had taken to verify Shin's story.<br /><br />As Harden relates, \\"North Korea's labor camps have now existed twice as long as the Soviet Gulag and about twelve times longer than the Nazi concentration camps. . . There are six camps, according to South Korea's intelligence agency and human rights groups. The biggest is thirty-one miles long and twenty-five miles wide, an area larger than the city of Los Angeles.\\"<br /><br />The author asks why there is so little awareness in the West about the North Korean camps, and why nothing has been done about them. As the Washington Post editorialized upon publishing Harden's first account of Shin's life: \\"High school students in America debate why President Franklin D. Roosevelt didn't bomb the rail lines to Hitler's camps. Their children may ask, a generation from now, why the West stared at far clearer satellite images of Kim Jong Il's camps, and did nothing.\\"<br /><br />Blaine Harden's journalistic credits include the Washington Post, the Economist, the New York Times, the New York Times Magazine, and PBS Frontline. He reported for many years from Africa, Eastern Europe, and Asia for the Washington Post, and it was on assignment to write the unwritten stories about North Korea for the Post that he encountered Shin Dong-hyuk.<br /><br />(From [...])e World Affairs Council of Northern California in a conversation with Philip Yun, Executive Director of the Ploughshares Fund, a former U.S. diplomat who is Korean-American and has studied the peninsula for many years. 014241543X|5.0|I've been letting this book sit in my head for the past few days because I really can't put my feelings about this book into words. So I'll settle for a very vague word: indescribable. If I Stay is purely indescribable.<br /><br />The characters in this novel could be my best friend, your best friend, someone's best friend's best friend ... They are so realistic that I think that if I tried to touch them, I'd feel living flesh. The complexity of these characters just astounds me. Mia is quiet, shy-but she's incredibly dedicated to playing cello. I don't often read music geeks in novels anymore, but I always love when I do. I'm a violin player myself, though I'm not very devoted to music; however, my best friend has been in love with her piano since she was 4. And since I don't have that kind of connection with my own music, I'm curious: I want to know what that special kind of happiness is. So far, I've only been able to compare it to my love of reading and writing. I'd say that it's this wonderful feeling that trembles in your body; it's something you can cherish and makes your heart burst. Utter joy.<br /><br />Mia's relationships with others are also incredibly unique, and you know that I'm a big fan of unique. She and her best friend, Kim (who is the queen of sarcasm), were originally mega-enemies; they didn't like each other just because everyone thought they should be best friends. But after one fight, they became instant buddies. Mia and her little brother, Teddy-who is absolutely adorable, let me tell you-are thick as thieves. And Mia and her parents are practically polar opposites; her dad was once a rock band drummer, and her mom is a total rocker chick. Obviously, with Mia's interest in classical music, their tastes clash. But the four of them make a hilarious family.<br /><br />Here is a description of the typical young adult book boyfriend: dark, steamy, handsome, hawt ... you get the point. They're supposed to knock you off your feet. So guess what theguy-Adam-does in this book?<br /><br />No, he doesn't knock you off your feet. But he is so incredibly sweet. Little things here and there just make you fall for him-like Mia did-before you even realize that you fell for him-like Mia realized. Their relationship is so un-clichéd that I want to leap for joy. Because they get in fights too. They have their differences too (Adam plays in a rock band). They are just a regular teenage couple-but the two of them are just so deeply in love I want to cry.<br /><br />But the part I loved the most about this book: the lesson. `Cause the cover pretty much sums it up with that one question: what would you do if you had to choose? If you were in a car accident, if your parents were dead, if your little brother might be on the verge of death, if you were stuck in a coma, if you knew that there was nothing for you if you came back except for a boyfriend and a best friend and a few relatives ... would you choose to live or die if you had the choice?<br /><br />It's a deep question. Right now you might say, \\"I'd choose to stay, of course,\\" but think about it. Take a deep breath and sit back and just think. You would be a seventeen-year-old orphan. Your life would definitely not be the same as it was before the accident. Would you really want to stay? Because, remember, you can't take back your choice. You're stuck with it.<br /><br />And before you say, \\"Of course Mia chooses to stay; I mean, after all, there is a sequel,\\" I'd like to point out that the sequel takes place from Adam's point of view three years later. But you might not want to read the synopsis for that, because it'll definitely spoil If I Stay.<br /><br />Deep and engrossing, If I Stay is a must for any reader. Such an emotional novel is guaranteed to kindle a response to the biggest choice one could possibly have: to live or to die.<br /><br />Source: Paperback received from giveaway

It is such a striking, powerful cue with tons of emotion.<br /><br />I highly recommend that you purchase the physical CD, but this album is also available as an MP3 purchase: [[ASIN:B008HI7BMW The Dark Knight Rises: Original Motion Picture Soundtrack]]<br /><br />EXCLUSIVE BONUS TRACKS:<br />* With the purchase of the physical CD, you can unlock two exclsuive bonus tracks online -- \\"No Stone Unturned\\" & \\"Risen From Darkness\\"<br />** iTunes also has two exclusive tracks -- \\"The Shadows Betray You\\" & \\"The End\\" (these are both spectacular, I purchased them from iTunes for $0.99 each)

034580404X|4.0|Can't give this the full five because of all the \\"Oh my!\\" \\"Holy crap!\\" and \\"His mouth presses into a thin hard line\\" repeated over and over. Ana gets a strong hand to guide her; I don't understand why E.L. James did not have the similarly strong hand of a good editor.<br /><br />In spite of this, I couldn't put 'em down, and I keep re-reading my favorite parts, trying to fathom why this series has struck such a primal chord with so many women, myself included.<br /><br />I suspect it goes back to mythology: in coming-of-age tales the world over, a boy's initiation into manhood requires that he conquer something. Sex is usually not part of that process, although he does have to learn to master his baser impulses along the way.<br /><br />But when the protagonist is a girl, her initiation into womanhood IS her first sexual experience. It's ALL about the sex. (See my video interview about the myth of Psyche, based on Paul Dobransky's theories, recorded before I read 50 Shades, on Julia Widdop's \\"Hero's Journey TV\\" site.<br /><br />At a deep, subconscious level, maybe women (no matter how old they get) long to be the vulnerable \\"maiden\\", experiencing the discovery of sex all over again. E.L.James' genius, if it can be called that, resides in Christian Grey being so sexually imaginative and inventive, that every time he does something with Ana, every time feels to her as if it's the first time. Maybe that's why the books \\"work\\" in spite of themselves.<br /><br />Give her credit too, for not making Ana a total pushover, in spite of her irresistible attraction to Christian. Feminists have been complaining that the depiction of their relationship pushes progress back by 50 years. But look again: In the face of Christian's overwhelming need for control, Ana fights to keep her boundaries intact, every step of the way. This is a psychological skill that all women must master (again, pun intended) in order to become emotionally mature.<br /><br />You might even consider it to be two coming-of-age tales in one: Parallel to Ana's constant redefining of her boundaries and her love, you have Christian's process of finding his own emotional maturity: mastering his baser impulses so his lust is elevated to love, without giving up his independence or masculinity, and still having just as much sex as both of them want, so they end up with a genuine glue that will sustain them through old age.<br /><br />At the end of the day, isn't that what most of us want? Men and women both?

B003I55BIA|4.0|I liked this book, but I wanted to love it (so three stars). Perfect Chemistry had been on my list for a long time, but for some reason I had managed not to read it. I kept coming back to it, looking at it, debating it, and probably building it up into something it just could never be. There were even times I was afraid of this book. How does that make sense? I grew up in a small town about two hours from Chicago in Illinois. Our town had a large Latino population and has a pretty bad gang problem. My school had a divide between those who have and those who have not. The lines were not clear and the lines were not necessarily racial. You could cross the lines in my little town and it really wasn't that big of a deal. Ah, so now you get why this story scared me. Could Simon Elkeles write a story so close to my reality and get it right? Could she do it without insulting me or my former community? I'm happy to say that for the most part, I did not feel insulted. There were times I had to laugh at some of the Spanish used and some of the over the top situations. There were also times I had to laugh about the way the rich (white only) kids spoke or acted in certain situations. I even had to text someone and ask if \\"people really thought that way,\\" which just showed that I only really understood one side of the community Ms. Elkeles created.<br /><br />Perfect Chemistry is a young adult romance novel. I have to mention this fact because I had to keep reminding myself what I was reading. Sometimes I wanted it to turn into a gritting novel about life in a divided community, but it wasn't that kind of book. It was a love story and a decent one at that. A story I wouldn't mind letting my pre-teenage daughter read a year or so from now (she's in jr high). As a teen romance, it is a good star crossed lover's story. The story has depth. The characters are three dimensional and I cared what happened to them.<br /><br />I believe I was nearly three quarters of the way through the book before I figured out that Alex had immigrated as a small child/baby. I'm not sure why this wasn't made clear or why it was even a part of the story. This small bit of information was almost insulting. It was as if most readers wouldn't or couldn't understand a Latino community unless it was full of immigrants. However, I didn't get the feel that all the kids from the \\"South Sided\\" were immigrants but I'm not sure if that was me or the writer projecting that onto the story. I'm not even sure why it bothered me, but it did, even though I knew plenty of people with this background. The Spanish was a little forced in some parts. I would have preferred the story to have more Spanglish, which would seem (to me) to be more common language for Alex and his friends.<br /><br />The gang stuff was almost comical. I had the impression that the only research about gang activity was obtained from watching Gangland on TV. Thankfully there wasn't enough of this content to make that big of an impact on the story. I will give Ms. Elkeles credit for not going too deep and for keeping things believable for most people. My biggest issue was Hector. At him all I can do is smile and shake my head.<br /><br />The rich kids were one dimensional to me. I looked at them as if they were the soc's from The Outsiders, but with more plot. Brittany, Cherry, the same person in a different time frame. Their clothes, their language, their cars, and their attitudes (especially the boys) all seem borrowed from the Outsiders. This isn't an issue for me though. I have probably read that book more times than anyone should admit publicly. It was a nice way to flesh out the characters, but I highly doubt anyone, more or less any group of people, are really like that. There were a lot of times I found myself asking my friends and sister if people really acted like these characters. I was shocked on one occasion to find that I was wrong and the situation I asked about was common around \\"naive girls thathave never been around poor people.\\" *scratch my head* \\"Really?\\"<br /><br />But I did like a lot about this story. I loved Alex! For the most part I understood the way his character thought and acted. As intended by Elkeles, Alex broke my heart at times. His hopelessness, his fear, his macho attitude, and his heart encompassed his situation perfectly. He's the intelligent gang member that could be anything in the world he wanted to be if it weren't for life getting in his way. I knew that guy, hell I knew several of him. That was what was great about the story, I put real faces with some of these characters. But the motorcycle was a little too much! Isa, our crossover character who bridged the gap between gangs and poms was hands down the most realistic character in this story for me. This is the person I knew in dozens of real life people. Paco was also a realistic character, so much so that I wanted to change his name in most scenes. Then there is Carmen. Oh Carmen, you scare me and you make me laugh in that good way. I liked her so very much and of all people in this story, I think I'm going to miss her most.<br /><br />Then there was Brittany. She was certainly believable. Didn't we all have that one girl in high school? That Regina George (Mean Girls reference), we all wanted to drop off the face of the earth? Who knew that she just wanted to wipe off the eyeliner, pull on some sweats, and fall in love with the motorcycle riding badass Mexican gang banger in the leather jacket (the Latino Fonzie)? But wait a minute she isn't Regina George, she's Cherry Vallens with a plotline! Her mom is crazy, she hates being \\"forced\\" into being \\"prefect\\", and she has thoughts and feelings. I wasn't expecting that! I loved how she cared for Shelly and how Brittany did everything because of how much she loved her sister. It really was a beautiful addition to the story. I don't know if her character was real, but I'm going to pretend she could exist. I'm not prepared to believe the Romeo and Juliet aspects, but THIS IS A ROMANCE NOVEL (again, said for me as a gentle reminder). I even liked her best friend Sierra. I want to believe her friend was pulling for her and could grow to like Alex. Is that too much to expect? Is this my personal prejudices showing, yet again? Probably. I really liked that little co-captain witch friend of hers. Darlene wasn't it? That one I can believe. Wait a minute, she was Regina George! I liked who Brittany became and I can appreciate her struggle even if I don't quite get it. I even like that she made mention of Sandy's sexy black outfit from Grease and then later the pom uniform thing. I like that Brittany tried it, even if it was stupid (her reasoning, not the seduction!).<br /><br />This story touched me. It made me think. It made me revisit a time and place I sometimes forget. I think Perfect Chemistry is going to stick with me for a while and I'm not even sure why. Maybe it isn't a three star book after all. Three stars cannot make me write an opus of a review. Four it is, I guess. The extra stars for making me want these things to be possible, for showing the world a piece of reality, and for selling the fantasy wholeheartedly.<br /><br />My favorite quotes:<br /><br />College is just a dream. Senior year for me is like a retirement party for a sixty-five-year-old. You know you can do more, but everyone expects you to quit. (pg 5)<br /><br />To Luis it's a bandanna. To me, it's a symbol of what is and will never be. (pg 7)<br /><br />I put on a hell of a show to the outside world; sometimes I even surprise myself. (pg 10)<br /><br />She has no clue how much my life outside of school leaks into the life I lead inside of school. A redbrick building can't shield me from the outside world. Hell, I couldn't hide in here even if I wanted to.(pg 64)<br /><br />...a white guy who lives on the south side, is Mexican even though he doesn't have any Mexican blood in his body. Or Latino blood, for that matter. Yet he's considered one of us. In Fairfield, where you were born defines who you are. (pg 72)<br /><br />I want to know how to make this girl laugh. I want to know what makes her cry. I want to know what it feels like to have her look at me as if I'm her knight in shining armor. 1451683405|3.0|I wanted to be convinced by the book, actually to explore the claims of \\"non-doership\\" in religious traditions such as Advaita Vedanta.  But I was left dissatisfied.  The experiments showing EEG registry before actual conscious awareness is more suggestive about something to wonder about.  But by itself, even at this level, it doesn't totally kill some version of the notion of \\"free will\\", though not in terms of micro behavior or singular behavior, but a whole series of behaviors in as in the description of \\"choices leads to habits, habits to character\\", in other words, an *emergent property* of what seems to be a self reflexive dynamic system that lends credence to the notion about responsibility and possibly \\"karma\\".<br /><br />On the level of philosophical argumentation, the main reason I'm not convinced by the book is that it's not clear to me what \\"free will\\" actually is, what it would mean independent of any condition, in the abstract. Such a notion would seem foreign to human life in the concrete. On the other hand, \\"cause and effect\\", or \\"chance\\" as an explanatory framework seems a priori not to leave any room \\"free will\\". If your explanatory framework is mechanical, then the conclusions cannot be otherwise than mechanical.<br /><br />We are then left with the notion of free will as a some vague social \\"useful fiction\\", something that one should not look too closely at, but we all have an intuitive sense of \\"roughly\\" what it means, even though we can't place our finger on exactly what it is.  This is the \\"man on the street\\", \\"common sense\\" meaning that may be useful in courts and child rearing.  But this is a different level discussion than than the level of mechanism.<br /><br />We don't oftentimes confuse discussions on art and literature with discussions on physics.  The frameworks for discourse for these disciplines are very different. It would be a travesty to explain a Beethoven symphony as simply vibrating sound waves at different frequencies. This also begs the question what is \\"real\\".  I suggest the same separation should be made for \\"free will\\".  This is not to deny that there will be \\"hard cases\\", places where both disciplines meet (e.g. brain tumor and changes in personality and behavior).  This reflects the inadequacy of our frameworks which are imperfect grasps at reality.  As the saying goes, \\"the map is not the territory\\".  But we can clear up some (not all) confusion by looking at \\"levels of discourse\\".<br /><br />A possible framework to begin to bridge the gaps between explanatory frameworks may be some form of \\"systems theory\\" with it's notion of \\"emergent properties\\".  As scientific understanding of biological \\"life\\" expands in the 21st century,  and if computing power continue to follow an exponential curve predicted by Ray Kurzweil,  the rise of self reflective artificial intelligence,  a possible understanding of  what can be termed \\"free will\\", even if as an epiphenomenon, may be possible.  These are possible glimpses of how the conversation can move forward in a scientific way.  We may live in interesting times on this question.

B001IBIHQ4|5.0|The Pacific is a documentary style mini-series that captures the essence of the epic struggle of the island fighting campaign during WWII.  Tom Hanks and Stephen Spielberg are to be complemented on bringing this story to life and doing it in such a way that the viewer can better understand just how horrific that struggle was.  As the story unfolds and we follow the lives of the characters as they face unimaginable situations, we the viewers are confronted with our own fears and are forced to ask...how would I have reacted under the same situations?  HBO has done an outstanding job in capturing these kinds of stories.  The mini series format is the only way to provide the kind of depth and character development this large of a story requires.  The Pacific is truly an epic not only in the telling of the Pacific war, but in bringing to life the characters who lived it.  Most other movies and even documentaries tend to dwell on the strategic efforts of the war.  This series tells the story of the individual GI...Marine...Sailor...the men who had to fight it...the men who had to die for it. It's their story placed within the context of actual events...times...and places. The historical accuracy is pretty good, even so...as a movie series, certain generalizations had to be made for story continuity and brevity.  I loved the way at the end how we were told how the lives of the characters continued on after the war...but what most impressed me about this series was how much better now can I visualize what my dad went through during his adventures on Okinawa.  Although I've always had great respect for our WWII veterans...that respect was elevated to a higher platform because of this series.

This biography was really well-rounded thanks to so many interviews with people he came in contact throughout his life, both good and bad, and you got the feeling that nothing was held back, but surprisingly little was given from Steve's wife, especially with regard to their marriage, so it would be really interesting to hear how she coped being married to him for 20-something years.<br /><br />Another thing I found really interesting was the role he played in both Apple and Pixar. After reading the book I realise he was a really great business executive, rather than an engineer, and he was able to make great imaginative leaps while valuing the end-to-end process of a product, and this is what sets Apple apart from its competitors. You really do need to admire his ability to evolve and advance technology, and in the book he often says that \\"people don't know what they want until I show them\\".<br /><br />I found the second part of the book more engaging as I was more familiar with the products and company. Steve seemed to be portrayed more harshly in the first half of the book.<br /><br />I would recommend this book for anyone who is a fan of technology or Steve Jobs, but it is not an easy biography to read if you are not a tech-head, and I found myself skim reading some paragraphs due to this. I'm glad I read it its but probably not one I would ever read again.

The frets are much too small for my average sized adult hands, but that is fine because I wanted it to fit hers.<br /><br />Unfortunately it has no value as an actual instrument. If your child just wants to strum some strings to make random noise then they will probably be happy. But as a guitar player myself, the first thing I tried to do was tune this and have it at least sound somewhat decent. Sadly, it simply cannot be tuned. The tuning pegs function in turning but as soon as you find the note you want and let go of the peg it unwinds itself enough that it is way out of key. I could not even get close to a tolerable tuning to play a single simple song that anyone would recognize. The strings are also strange, they have the appearance of bronze but when I tried to tune one up to a higher note it snapped easier than a nylon string. I was able to sort of repair it since the rest of the instrument doesn't actually function so no one will notice but all in all, for such a cute looking instrument it was a big disappointment.<br /><br />If you want a sturdy and cute toy just for fun then this is inexpensive and cute enough and to please any little girl but if you want a guitar with any real musical value, this is not it.<br /><br />**update**<br />Just broke a second string and they are unique to this guitar so I cannot simply replace the string like a normal one. It's headed for the trash, I am afraid.

B000O8OTNC|1.0|I don't see how this received so many good reviews!  I'd like to see EVERY reviewer describe the item and blade thickness in their reviews for reference. I've seen some reviews for sharpening scissors, which is different.  I tried the fine v notch on a Leatherman multi-tool and it did NOT improve it's sharpness, but made it more dull because it cut too much into the blade. A sharpening stone works MUCH better, but you need a round file for serrated edges.  Cons: The diamond coating on the rod does not last very long. The rod mount is wimpy and easily breaks. The carbide v notch is ONLY for repairing thicker/heavily damaged blades!  The angle of the blade edge determines sharpness and longevity. The sharper you want it, the more often you will have to sharpen it. Obviously steel quality affects durability as well.  Compare blade thickness, sharpness and angle to an axe and a razor blade. Axes are more durable, but not 'razor' sharp. Razors are very sharp, not durable. They are the smallest degree angle possible.  Failed on my paper/arm hair test after using this! This sharpener just doesn't 'CUT' it! I went back to sharpening stones; so much easier.  For in depth information on blades and sharpening, look up Straight Razor on Wikipedia/other.

0393338827|2.0|I picked up this book because I wanted to read something new and different, and I could check it out for free with Amazon Prime.  I didn't know anything about the world of finance, but from the rave reviews about the author's wit and writing ability, I thought I might learn something and be amused at the same time--a great combination!  I did learn something, which is why I gave the book two stars instead of one, but the book failed to amuse.<br /><br />As an author, I believe that people who write books ought to have enough vocabulary to communicate their point without profanity. This book disappointed me on that score.  I also needed better explanations for words and phrases commonly used in the finance world that lack meaning to the rest of society.  As a storyteller, I found the narrative repetitive, lacking in cohesion, and without good structure.  The author introduces several characters and gives descriptions and personal information about their lives and their participation with Wall Street, but he repeated himself to the point that sometimes I felt like I'd read the same paragraph or page in an earlier section.  He finally began to weave the narratives together and build tension, and the book gained interest.  Then he brought the flow of the book to a screeching halt by going back to introduce yet another character: one Howie Hubler, who \\"grew up in New Jersey and payed football at Montclair State College.\\"  Honestly, I didn't care, and I didn't want to have to wade through yet another rendition of the greed and avarice of Wall Street before the crash. 0307956350|3.0|Kate is a woman with a secret. For the decade of her marriage, she's been a CIA operative... but she hasn't seen fit to tell her husband. Dexter, her adorably clueless computer genius husband, has bought her story about working for the State department hook line and sinker. She always felt a little guilty for lying to him, though, so when he gets a chance of a lifetime job in Luxembourg, she jumps at the chance to get out of The Company. But then another American expatriate (the Expats, natch) couple enters their life, and something just smells fishy...<br /><br />First off, let me say that I'll be surprised if this book hasn't already been optioned for a movie. I'm not saying that the story is all that (though it isn't awful), but this is a very visual book, and the action scenes are particularly well written. The characters are sketched clearly, very stock, and Hollywood might see some possibility of drawing in female viewers to an action movie, as it would feature 'one of their own'(think Stephanie Plum, but more competent and far less funny). Maybe it would work in film form.<br /><br />As a book, though, it has issues. The very 'stock' nature of the characters that make them perfect for the broad strokes of film make them unbelievable in a book. I never felt connected to Kate, the stock bored SAHM; it's not that I can't understand where she's coming from, it's that the descriptions of what bores her and how she feels could have been cut and pasted from many other books. Pavone has some nice turns of phrase, but he never quite 'gets' the women he's writing. Those sections that deal with social and family matters drag--in fact, though a key past action in the book hinges on Kate's supposed 'mother love', there's very little indication of her having much at all to do with her kids in the now. They are little cyphers, going from playground to preschool; I had no picture of them at all. The interactions between the women in the book are similarly shallowly drawn. I would never say that men can never write a primary female protagonist (or the opposite, for that matter), but it takes a deft touch to make those characters ring true. The narrative device of jumping from Luxembourg to 'today'(complete with time of day defined) sometimes worked and sometimes did not. At times the time flip served to advance the narrative or inform the reader, but at times it was just jarring. Again, I think this might go over better in a film. The most unbelievable thing to me is how very blind Kate is to what is patently obvious from the beginning of the story. I don't want to have spoilers in this review, but her refusal to see what is right before her eyes--repeatedly--was a bit unbelievable. It leads the reader to think that she must have been a singularly unsuccessful spy, but we're told repeatedly how good she was at her job. I've never read a character so determined not to trust her instincts or her reason.<br /><br />Where Pavone excels is in his action scenes. They are crisply written, exciting, and very clear. I'm a very visual reader, so I appreciate it when I can see the 'blocking' of a scene in my head on the first read through and it all makes sense. In this area, he gives Tom Clancy at his best a run for his money.<br /><br />Although I think The Expats misses the mark more often than it hits the darn thing, I was able to finish it. I probably wouldn't read this book again, but I'd try something else by Pavone (preferably centered on action and with a male protagonist).would never say that men can never write a primary female protagonist (or the opposite, for that matter), but it takes a deft touch to make those characters ring true. It makes me wonder how in the heck she got an executive job.<br /><br />Ok...about the book: Sex, sex, sex, and little plot.  This is my first erotica book and I found myself wanting more of a storyline.  At first the erotic scenes were hot but then they became repetitive like the rest of the book. I wanted to find out more about Christian in the first book and wanted to find out how he felt about her (Ana) but the ending entailed him beating the shit out of her (Ana) with a belt because she wanted to see \\"how bad it could be\\". Then finally she stood up for herself and left him. As a strong woman myself...I decided I liked that ending and decided not to read the other books. I am a hopeless romantic and \\"The Notebook\\" is my favorite book which is also an easy read but not to the degree of simplicity and repetitive nature of this book.<br /><br />Am I mad I wasted my time reading the book...no atleast I know what all the hype is aboutnow.  It did make me realize how lucky I am that I have my loving husband so I gave it an extra star for that. I won't bother reading the other two books...I will just google the ending to find out instead of reading through all of the repetitive jargon and lip biting, frowning, smirking, etc. I don't have much time to read...I have 2 small children and I work so if/when I do read a book, the next one will have more of a storyline.<br /><br />I don't recommend buying the book...borrow it or get it at the library if you want to know what all the fuss is about.

B008645YEE|5.0|[Arggg....this is no less than the 6th time I've re-edited this review. Bottom line: it's growing on me! ***]<br /><br />Definitely the weakest of the trilogy scores -- IMO -- was 2008's ADK. TDKR, OTOH, returns to many of the emotional elements I found attractive in BB. Curiously, this time there is no JNH co-composing the score. As much as I like JNH's solo scores, I'm wondering whether some of the rumors about him being the \\"junior contributor\\" to Batman scores are true? **<br /><br />Back to TDKR ... and on to a sort-of \\"review\\" of the album-version score....<br /><br />I like the precociousnesses, SERIOUSNESS and darkness of the Nolan-trilogy scores -- and maybe that comes with the territory. I think Zimmer can competently do light-hearted scores (PoC, Sherlock Holmes) but they are not for me because that genre is personally not interesting.<br /><br />IMO, best by far, is bonus trk \\"The end\\". Very ambient, low-key, haunting, precocious and MOVING... none of that tiresome and now-overused 'Zimmer percussion' (loved it ORIGINALLY in BB's Molossus, but when it is recycled over and over, things get boring). \\"The End\\" is a MATURE conclusion, and ranks up there with the best Nolan-trilogy tracks.<br /><br />I should note that while I'm a huge filmscore fan, I'm not a film buff. Even tho' critics and audiences found Nolan's saga respectable, I could not sit thru more than an hour into the DVD. Indeed, I collapsed the video window in my PC's DVD player, worked on other things while I simply LISTENED to the film on headphones ... all while doing other tasks on the PC ... the scores are way more mature: In fact, the OSTs (even at their weakest), to me, almost seem out of place -- i.e., precocious, serious (which the movies' characters -- along with acting efforts, visuals,plot and story -- simply aren't). So this maturity mis-match makes one wonder how much HZ (and JNH, other composers in other STs) DELIBERATELY use ample movie-industry $/resources to push their pure-stand-alone art? I know Jerry Goldsmith commented on this issue in the Hollow-Man DVD suppl. commentary audio track.<br /><br />Anyway... whatever it takes to get GOOD art out .... including $250 million budgets !!<br /><br />NOTES:<br />**  One can wonder if JNH + HZ would have made this a better OST -- or might it be a Zeitgeist thing (e.g., a right combo of being at the right place at the right time, etc., etc., resulting in projects that really click together)? An example of this is 2005's BB -- IMO, a superb collaborative effort, and the best score of the three. I thought TDK**** pretty much fell apart although the two composers were involved; I like HZ's solo effort TDKR much, much better than TDK. Given these \\"inconsistencies\\"/unpredictabilities, at least as far as my opinions are concerned, I can't extrapolate any \\"rules-of-thumb\\" other than generalities:<br />-HZ is good.<br />-JNH is good.<br />-Listen to all stuff they release.<br />-Judge each project INDIVIDUALLY.<br />I thought JNH's Snow White/Huntsman had more better tracks than DKR, but DKR's \\"The End\\" may be my fave OST track of this year (thus far).] [EDIT 2012-07-24_a: I've changed my mind: on this: extended listening to TDKR now tips the favor to it over JNH's Snow White. Like I said above, TDKR seems to -- for one reason or another -- be favorably \\"growing on me\\". ]<br /><br />*** I AM NOT A FILM BUFF. I AM NOT EVEN YOUR ORDINARY-JOE MOVIE WATCHER. I have not seen the DKR film, nor am I planning on seeing it theatrically. I may watch it on home video one day, but a home watch of BB (if you've read my BB OST review) was quite disappointing. Not all films I've watched has there been such a qualitative discordance between the score and the rest-of-the-film. Nolan's Batman trilogy -- apart from its score, of course -- remains (IMO) the usual, immature, appeal-to-the-mass, overrated tripe.<br /><br />****Putting things in perspective: Even tho' I consider 2008's TDK the weakest of the three Nolan-trilogy scores, it's WAY, WAY better than most OSTs by other artists. All IMO, of course ;)!!

0786958731|5.0|Who am I:<br />Middle aged married guy who loves Sci fi, video games and fantasy novels. Used to play D&D and then AD&D around twenty years ago. Haven't been involved with any serious groups since then so I have no experience with any editions of D&D that cause such a rift in the community. This means that I am not hopelessly in love with any specific Edition of D&D.<br /><br />-Note for various D&D Edition FanGirls and FanBoys- If you are a 4th Edition Hater and can't get past that, you most likely will not enjoy these games as they are based, in general, on the 4th Edition rule set. If you don't know what that statement means, or if you are capable of enjoying what each Edition has to offer, you should have no problems.<br /><br />Why I love the D&D Adventure Systems:<br />Castle Ravenloft(CR), Wrath of Ashardalon(WoA) and Legend of Drizzt(LoD): They work as a stand alone product and offer a dungeon crawling, limited leveling, monster slaying experience with no previous knowledge of how D&D works. And all in sessions that can be finished in an hour or two. The minimal time commitment involved is fantastic.<br /><br />Or, if you find yourself filled with nostalgia and desire a fuller, longer campaign with further leveling options or new characters, these games can be tweaked to allow for that. Especially with some of the user created content online, the photoshop templates on boardgamegeek.com in particular are wonderful. Also this [....]<br />address has a plethora of new content. The extra leveling options are definitely my favorite. The truth is that I screamed in happiness when I ran across them. I sounded like a ten year old girl getting a pony. A unicorn pony. with wings. A Unicorn Pegasus Pony that appeared in a shimmer of rainbow colors. And now I am deeply ashamed...........*sigh* I am such a nerd.<br /><br />The game system totally allows for expandability in many ways. It's kind of a gateway drug for D&D honestly. I find myself consistently wanting just a bit more of the actual RPG experience. We tweak the game a bit and suddenly our experience is just a bit fuller and more satisfying. Not nearly as detailed and time intensive as an actual D&D or Pathfinder game though. Such a great balance for us.<br /><br />They don't have to be expanded upon though, to be immensely enjoyable. Any of the three games are enough in and of themselves to be hugely gratifying and offer much re-playability. The expandability is just something that makes them extra cool and versatile in my opinion.<br /><br />All three systems use totally interchangeable materials. Some of the cards may be context sensitive and not seem to fit in a particular environment but other than that you can use everything in each of the games. This provides Awesomeness Bonus of +2.<br /><br />The map tiles and figurines both are an unbeatable value and can be easily incorporated into a \\"real\\" D&D session.<br />Everything in the box, other than the manuals, is of superb quality. The figurines alone would easily cost you double what you pay for the set here. And if you paint figurines, which I do, you will have just gained around forty new minis to paint. Joy in and of itself there.<br /><br />Possible Cons:<br />I saw in the review for one of the three games that some dude was just totally disgusted with how these games \\"punish\\" the players for almost every activity they engage in. The guy was totally a Hater. However, he wasn't necessarily wrong. The game does seem to be constantly throwing things at you that often don't feel fair. Notably, the Encounter cards. Which brings me to the next con and a solution to the \\"punishment\\" issue....<br /><br />The instruction manual will not answer every question that will come up. It is left up to the players to decide how certain things should work. Which is how a \\"real\\" D&D game works. Your group plays the game the way they want to play it. If you simply cannot function without every rule spelled out for you, this may bea problem. Otherwise you'll be fine. And in terms of rule tweaking and the punishment issue- we decided to change how often we draw encounter cards as well as a couple other minor aspects of the game. This has served to make our sessions a lot more fun. Don't be afraid to tweak the rules in order to play the game how you want to play it.<br /><br />The manuals are pretty cheaply put together. They may not hold up well to use by younger players or spilled drinks. I took ours apart and put the pages in plastic binder inserts. We now have all the material for all the games in a single, sturdy, custom decorated notebook. Which is awesome btw. (Disclaimer for claim of awesomeness: I created the custom artwork:)<br /><br />Space. With all the cards and map tiles and bags of monsters and tokens that get used in a typical session, you may find you need a bit more space than an average board game. All of the aforementioned items can quickly end up being a mess. As a solution we have everything securely contained in varying sizes of ziplock bags. Including the map tiles in a 1 Gallon size bag. Perfect for storage and during play.<br /><br />There is no actual role playing or in town scenarios. If you want to get into a brawl over the serving wench's honor at The Yawning Portal Inn in the city of Waterdeep, then you should just find a gaming group and play Pathfinder or D&D(any Edition). Or perhaps, after stumbling into a gathering of Dirty Orcs in the Gloomy Forest of Gloom and Terror, you want to attempt to use an illusion to convince them you are a deity and sell them into slavery in the Land of Thay. As opposed to just killing them. Again, just go find a full-on game of D&D. These systems can be worked to allow for things like that to some degree, but they aren't designed to be open world or allow for anything other than a dungeon crawl with combat and a final, combat based, objective.<br /><br />So:<br />If you want \\"real\\" D&D, then you may be more satisfied finding a group and getting involved.<br />If you want a game that allows you to engage in D20 based combat, with figurines, that represent a character with minimal leveling capacity. And that can be played by anyone. These systems may be just the thing for you.<br /><br />Also of note: The Dungeon Command games have interchangeable map tiles, monster cards and figurines that can be used seamlessly with the Adventure Systems. I love this as it ads even more expansion options.a problem. Otherwise you'll be fine. And in terms of rule tweaking and the punishment issue- we decided to change how often we draw encounter cards as well as a couple other minor aspects of the game. This has served to make our sessions a lot more fun. Don't be afraid to tweak the rules in order to play the game how you want to play it.<br /><br />The manuals are pretty cheaply put together. We now have all the material for all the games in a single, sturdy, custom decorated notebook. Which is awesome btw. (Disclaimer for claim of awesomeness: I created the custom artwork:)<br /><br />Space. All of the aforementioned items can quickly end up being a mess. As a solution we have everything securely contained in varying sizes of ziplock bags. Including the map tiles in a 1 Gallon size bag. Perfect for storage and during play.<br /><br />There is no actual role playing or in town scenarios. I love this as it ads even more expansion options.

B004LWZWGA|5.0|Christopher Nolan puts the human element front and center, taking the cartoonish out of super heroes and making \\"The Dark Night Rises\\" seem a lot shorter than 2 ½ hours. The aches and pains of being a super hero or super villain one wouldn't think compelling but in the hands of Nolan and an all-star cast it's riveting. Nolan is producing Superman \\"Man of Steel\\" for 2013 release. I can't wait.<br /> Erstwhile political commentator Rush Limbaugh saw something nefarious in a \\"Dark Night\\" villain being named Bane in the same year that the Republican presidential nominee is a Bain Capital alumnus. Leaving aside that Bane dates back in the comic books to 1993 and that Nolan and Daniel Goyer wrote their script in 2010, Rush is right that the film may do damage to neoconservatives/establishment Republicans but not in the way that he thinks. The fact that Bane's act of love and protection is the reason for his damaged face/mask does damage to the neocons' simplistic view of evil. That Batman, too, wears a mask because of love and acts of protection and factoring out megalomania and unconcern for collateral damage that he's very much like Bane (they both had the same mentor) is more firepower directed at the unthinking neocon intellectual edifice. Yes, Rush, be afraid, be very afraid.<br /> Perhaps Rush can relax because \\"The Dark Knight Rises\\" is the third in Dolan's trilogy although I got the sense that this excellent movie will be seen historically as the end of the beginning. At film's end Detective Blake is ready to pick up the mantle as Robin/Nightwing. Nobody calls him either name until five minutes before closing credits but those with soul to see can tell early on that Blake is on his way to Robinhood.<br /> Gary Oldman (\\"Tinker, Tailor, Soldier, Spy\\") is at his usual tortured excellence as Commissioner Gordon, hiding the terrible secret of District Attorney Harvey Dent's descent into Two-Face madness for the good of Gotham City. Batman/Bruce Wayne (Christian Bale)goes along to his own psychological detriment/physical endangerment.<br /> Dent's legacy reminds us of the double-sidedness of the law - essential for social order yet an instrument of oppression when taken too far (as rigorous enforcement of drug laws today falling disproportionately on the poor demonstrates). Glory-seeking by some police is given respectful, honest treatment.<br /> Conservatives will appreciate the treatment Nolan and Goyer give Bane's \\"power-to-the people\\" revolution. That thievery and kangaroo courts are the main offspring of \\"people's\\" movements is not something that should surprise any serious student of history although these tendencies are neatly camouflaged by the \\"civilization\\" we take for granted. Bravo to the script writers for their astuteness about the human psyche.<br /> Additional kudos for leaving off the super hero titles which further displays the brokenness of the people. Bruce Wayne doesn't don the Batman suit until at least 30 minutes into the movie. At his heels is Alfred (Michael Caine) pleading for Bruce to help Gotham in a different, more lasting way. Alfred issues a Clubber Lang-type (see \\"Rocky III\\") warning about Bane that is ignored and their friendship isn't the only thing that gets broken as a result. Selina Kyle is a cynical, reluctant heroine as Catwoman. The fact that she isn't labeled Catwoman makes us listen to her heart.<br /> That Noland and Goyer didn't just retell the DC Comics classic \\"Knightfall\\" tale (available in graphic novel and hardcover book) is in keeping with The New 52 movement launched by DC last year. Both endeavors take pieces from the classical canon and arrange them in new and compelling ways. As the folks at Bain Capital might say, this adds value.<br /> Nolan hits it out of the park with a late plot twist revealing that Bane is not the central villain and that the central villain has been hiding in plain sight. Backs are important in this movie - I urge you to study them. The body language communicates itself subtly but assuredly - a pretty face or a mask can hide an evil mind and the key to combating wickedness is to understand what's gone before (or at your back as some might say) and stand boldly for your truth.

It is left up to the players to decide how certain things should work. Which is how a \\"real\\" D&D game works. Your group plays the game the way they want to play it. If you simply cannot function without every rule spelled out for you, this may bea problem. Otherwise you'll be fine. And in terms of rule tweaking and the punishment issue- we decided to change how often we draw encounter cards as well as a couple other minor aspects of the game. This has served to make our sessions a lot more fun. Don't be afraid to tweak the rules in order to play the game how you want to play it.<br /><br />The manuals are pretty cheaply put together. They may not hold up well to use by younger players or spilled drinks. I took ours apart and put the pages in plastic binder inserts. We now have all the material for all the games in a single, sturdy, custom decorated notebook. Which is awesome btw. (Disclaimer for claim of awesomeness: I created the custom artwork:)<br /><br />Space. With all the cards and map tiles and bags of monsters and tokens that get used in a typical session, you may find you need a bit more space than an average board game. All of the aforementioned items can quickly end up being a mess. As a solution we have everything securely contained in varying sizes of ziplock bags. Including the map tiles in a 1 Gallon size bag. Perfect for storage and during play.<br /><br />There is no actual role playing or in town scenarios. If you want to get into a brawl over the serving wench's honor at The Yawning Portal Inn in the city of Waterdeep, then you should just find a gaming group and play Pathfinder or D&D(any Edition). Or perhaps, after stumbling into a gathering of Dirty Orcs in the Gloomy Forest of Gloom and Terror, you want to attempt to use an illusion to convince them you are a deity and sell them into slavery in the Land of Thay. As opposed to just killing them. Again, just go find a full-on game of D&D. These systems can be worked to allow for things like that to some degree, but they aren't designed to be open world or allow for anything other than a dungeon crawl with combat and a final, combat based, objective.<br /><br />So:<br />If you want \\"real\\" D&D, then you may be more satisfied finding a group and getting involved.<br />If you want a game that allows you to engage in D20 based combat, with figurines, that represent a character with minimal leveling capacity. And that can be played by anyone. These systems may be just the thing for you.<br /><br />Also of note: The Dungeon Command games have interchangeable map tiles, monster cards and figurines that can be used seamlessly with the Adventure Systems. I love this as it ads even more expansion options.a problem. Otherwise you'll be fine. 0307592731|5.0|Reviewing this book is a bit like reviewing your best friend, or the love of your life.  It's pretty hard to be objective when something speaks to the deepest, truest part of you, and then changes you forever.  Wild did that for me.<br />Wild is one woman's coming of age story.  In a society where we don't have a ritual to help young people learn what it is to be an adult, to trust their instincts, and to find and honor their strengths and weaknesses, Strayed creates her own.  In the summer of 1995, at 26 years old, reeling from the death of her mother, the slow dissolving of her family, and the failure of her marriage, Strayed commits herself 1,100 mile trek on the high-altitude Pacific Crest Trail, solo, repeating her mantra the whole way:  I am not afraid.  Along with her 60+ pound pack, she carries with her a broken heart and devastating mistakes, heavy burdens to be sure.  And while this book talks about those burdens, they are never about them more than they are the trip itself.  Unlike Eat Pray Love (which Wild resembles in the sense that is about a woman's search for meaning) Strayed's voice is not neurotic, frantic, or laced with self pity.  Strayed's is the voice of someone who has walked slowly over the bed of hot coals (barefoot) and stands at the other side, transformed.  Contrary to Eat Pray Love, there is no happy ending here, as there shouldn't be.  No one is ever relieved of the burden of suffering by any other person or by achieving a goal, Strayed seems to say, we just grow strong enough to bear it, and come to find comfort in its weight.<br />For me, that is what makes this book a masterpiece.  Strayed doesn't shy away from the truth, which is known to all of us in our heart of hearts:  life is not easy.  It's hard.  It's a long, hard, impossible journey that none of us are ever prepared for and, as crappy as it seems, there may be no reward for completing the journey.  So, she seems to say in Wild, make the journey something spectacular.  Make it something fine and true and real.  Make it something so wild that you will be proud, when you walk out of the wilderness at the other end, to have it called it your life.

B000K4X5XA|3.0|Though a bit corny at times, this movie really holds up well. Great cast and script, classic John Williams score and innovative special effects. Even with the barrage of great superhero movies that have come out over the past decade, 34-year-old \\"Superman: The Movie\\" remains one of the best.<br /><br />As far as this Blu-Ray is concerned, however, I'm not very impressed. Compared to VHS and 90's television broadcasts, this offers a lot of improvements. Most notably would be the color corrections on \\"flying\\" shots, but these updates were already available on DVD. In this edition there is grain across the board, and the colors and contrast look highly compressed, as if they were intended for a medium-bandwidth broadcast. If you're watching on a projector like reviewer \\"Achilles\\", you might not notice the contrast issues, but anyone with a recent LCD or Plasma television is going to see grey blocks where there should be black as well as narrow color bands. The audio quality is also disappointing, especially with dialogue. Fuzzy and undefined, lacking the clarity of other 70's film transfers.<br /><br />Movie: 9/10<br />Video: 7/10<br />Audio: 7/10<br /><br />If you don't already have a DVD version of this film, I'd say go ahead and get this Blu-Ray. If you DO already have a previous edition, I'd hold off: \\"Man of Steel\\" is coming out in June of 2013, which means you can bet a MUCH better quality release of the 1978 film will be appearing soon, probably early 2013.

Some might immediately compare the sound to GTR, but these power chords and rhythms speak for themselves.<br /><br />Chris Squire has earned his reputation as a sterling bassist.  A listener can find themselves falling into his engaging, addictive bass lines.  They don't distract, but add so much to the melody  He and Hackett provide lush, layered lead vocals and harmonies to the mix.<br /><br />Hackett's guitar work outs are an aural treat that fill out the songs beautifully.  Whatever atmosphere the song employs, Hackett's guitar work seems to grow organically out of the melody.<br /><br />\\"Tall Ships\\" chugs along with a walking, funky bass bottom over a light, bouncy tune.  It's got a great groove with  in-the-pocket drumming from Jeremy Stacy and lead vocals from Squire.<br /><br />Both Squire and Hackett like powerful rhythms and this CD has plenty of that.<br />The drumming by Jeremy Stacy never overwhelms the music, but keeps the beat solid through some powerful rhythms.  Stacy also adds a nice, light touch to the more airy melodies.<br /><br />Roger King adds just the right balance of keyboards to songs.  The man blends in his sound with whatever the music requires.  He also engineered and produce the album.<br /><br />The songs feature some wonderful high-energy Hackett solos.<br /><br />Squire begins \\"Stormchaser\\" with a chunky, bass line full of funk and even takes a turn with a bass solo.<br /><br />They offer an island-flavored change of pace with, \\"Can't Stop the Rain.\\"  Songs like \\"Divided Self\\" and \\"Sea Of Smiles\\" are catchy tunes with strong hooks.  It becomes hard not to sing along to the indelible choruses here.  The latter track is now available as a download on itunes.<br /><br />There are no tricky time changes to throw the listener off; just some, good  music to tap your toe to.<br /><br />The CD ends with \\"A Perfect Love Song\\" with a chorus that continues to swim around in your head long after the song has ended.<br /><br />Overall, this is a exceptional collections of songs from two of rock's best artists.  You don't have to be a progressive-rock fan to like this.  You just have to like good music.

B00843TZJ2|5.0|CSN has not exactly flooded the market with new releases - the latest release of the 3 together being the CSNY release of \\"Deja Vu Live\\" which was extremely Neilocentric, to say the least. So for all fans this is a very welcome occasion to hear an official, brilliantly recorded concert.<br /><br />And thankfuly they deliver a profound evening, mixing the old classics with newer songs, including Graham Nash's controversial and poignant tribute to Bradley Manning \\"Almost Gone\\". And diving deep into their impressive back catalogue, specially Stephen Stills who come up with the rarely performed \\"As I Come Of Age\\" and \\"So Begins The Task\\" (both played at the CSNY 1970 tour). Especially the first is truly magic, providing one of the moments of transcendent (vocal) beauty that is unique for this trio.<br /><br />As for the backingband it's almost the same as on the [[ASIN:B005I4HNTY Crosby-Nash: In Concert]] with the addition of Todd Caldwell on the Hammond B-3. a band who knows how to respectfully lay back but also can rock with the best of them all anchored by the drumming of Steve DiStanislao (known for his work on David Gilmour's \\"Island\\"-album and following tour).<br /><br />Much malign has been said about Stills's vocal, but actually his singing is better here than it has been in many years really enabling him to hit the higher notes. And his legendary guitarplaying as firery and riveting as ever. Graham Nash - probably one of the all time most underrated songwriters - and David Crosby still delivers their trademark harmony vocals.<br /><br />This band has for whatever reasons always attracted a certain animosity and some decisevely unfair criticism. But they also have a dedicated fan base and this DVD clearly shows the strong bond - and deep rapport - between band and audience.<br /><br />Sure the day when they were the vanguard is long gone, but what they deliver 2012 is very far from nostalgia and cash-in. So their broad and dedicated fan base should delight in this. And for new fans/listeners this might be the perfect introduction to one of the most adventurous and prolific bands ever.is. And for new fans/listeners this might be the perfect introduction to one of the most adventurous and prolific bands ever.

B005D0RDO8|5.0|I don't care so much about films like \\"Saw\\" or \\"Hostel\\". Maybe these films fit in our desensitized times, maybe we simply don't care that much anymore. We have Canadian porn killers and Batman massacres these days, all turned into huge media spectacles. In my opinion, the one and only true film about the state of humanity is Pasolini's last ever film. \\"Salò, or the 120 Days of Sodom\\" is a destructive masterpiece, anything but feel-good but extremely important. It's easy to focus on the torture and killings, but I guess the true horror of the film lies in the disturbing reactions of the youngsters. The victims betray each other, the four high ranking government officials' helpers don't care about what's going on. Desensitized behaviour is what Pasolini shows us here. People who call this 'the most grotesque film ever made' have no idea what they're talking about. Pasolini does not sensationalize sex or murders. The film offers an aesthetic detachment. It's anything but grotesque, it's a classic film. An uncomfortable experience it is, nevertheless. But then again: the truth is always hard to swallow. It's mostly hard to accept and most often unpleasant. Don't blame the messenger.

B004LWZWGK|4.0|Gotham has, against all odds, become a prosperous metropolis, a safer place to be. The Commissioner's anti-crime Dent Act has put a serious crimp on organized crime. It's been eight years since the Batman was last seen, eight years since he was accused of murdering Harvey Dent and the Commissioner allowed the lie in order to serve the greater good. Except there is no dearth of cowardly, superstitious lots. On the rooftops of Gotham and in the secret dens of the priveleged, an elusive cat burglar begins to prowl. And, compelled by a terrifying agenda, the zealot Bane begins to exert his insidious influence over Gotham City. It's been eight years since the Batman deemed his work done. But today his city is once more threatened. Retirement's over-rated.<br /><br />Never mind that one dude snoring away in the corner of the movie theater late Sunday night. Everyone else walked away fairly impressed. Christopher Nolan has done a wonder. THE DARK KNIGHT RISES caps off one of the best ever cinematic trilogies. Put it right up there with the original Star Wars trilogy and the Lord of the Rings and the Indiana Jones and Toy Story franchises. Is it perfect? No. THE DARK KNIGHT RISES is a tour-de-force but with a few flaws, a masterpiece with scuff marks. It's still unmissable.<br /><br />Eight years after the horrific climax of THE DARK KNIGHT, we catch up with a hobbled Bruce Wayne. Wracked with guilt over his inability to save Harvey Dent and Rachel Dawes, Bruce has abandoned the Bat suit. Once regarded as Gotham's favorite son, Bruce has become an eccentric recluse, his wealth steadily seeping away. This allows Michael Caine's faithful butler Alfred to deliver several emotional motivational speeches. And maybe one of them finally resonates with Bruce. He straps on a brace on his busted leg and steps out. Whispers of a monster named Bane circulate, and Bruce, who had shied away from his base of operations, ventures down to the Batcave. Good thing he'd long ago installed an advanced copy of Windows 2010, because, even after eight years of neglect, the Batcave's computer system is still cutting edge stuff.<br /><br />Okay, plot SPOILER in the last sentence of this next paragraph.<br /><br />Nolan applies elements of BATMAN: YEAR ONE, KNIGHTFALL, and NO MAN'S LAND to his narrative; longtime Batman readers can draw certain plot points from that. This film's scope is amazing. Nolan takes advantage of his near three-hours-long running time to tell a story that is rich and sprawling and occasionally breathtaking, never mind that the staff of JAMA and the New England Journal of Medicine are probably rolling their eyes at how rapidly Bruce resolves his grievous medical ailments in such a constrained timeline. Unless, of course, you really can remedy a broken back just by pushing a protruding vertebra back into the spine.<br /><br />Nolan is masterful with the technical bits, from cinematography to sound to score to use of the color palette to help establish mood. We're watching a great director at the height of his powers. Let's enjoy this guy. The acting performances, as expected, are across-the-board superb. There were doubts going in about Anne Hathaway as Selina Kyle, but remember that people also scoffed when Heath Ledger was cast as the Joker. Hathaway pulls it off. She makes a convincing Catwoman, although no one ever calls her that in the film. I like her character arc, although it's an obvious one. She lends depth to the role, serves as a fantastic foil for Batman. I love Selina's quicksilver personality shifts and that she's razor sharp with her delivery, and that she comes across as equally playful but dangerous. Hathaway's is miles ahead of Halle Berry and Michelle Pfeiffer's interpretations. Hells, she may even be the film's breakout character, although Joseph Gordon-Levitt's resourceful rookie cop also effortlessly commands the screen.<br /><br />In the excessive neck snapping portion of the discussion, I predict that Tom Hardy'sominous big bad will be the most slighted performance. But Hardy exudes presence, and, contrary to the early trailer, I understood most of his dialogue. But why did Bane have to go out with a whimper? He deserved a bigger finish.<br /><br />THE DARK KNIGHT RISES isn't as good as THE DARK KNIGHT. How could it be? As solid as this cast is, no one reaches the sublime heights of Ledger's Joker. But this is a deserving finale that, judged on its own merits, achieves its own measure of brilliance. Nolan had \\"epic\\" in mind when he closed the books on his franchise. His Batman is traumatized and gets put thru several kinds of hell. Even Frank Miller wants to give him a hug.<br /><br />There are scenes heavy on exposition, yeah, but only rarely did I feel they were slow.
