B0027MJU00|5.0|I've heard the words often in reviews: wonderful, beautiful, mesmerizing, etc, etc, etc. Adjective after adjective of positive qualification heaped upon the word prose. To be honest, I'm an avid reader that's never really appreciated that word, regardless of how many adjectives smothered it. I've only really understood beauty as it's related to poetry, and even then, only rarely. But Gillian Flynn really, truly deserves every one of the adjectives thrown at her prose. It's hard to even find the right words to describe the way her metaphors and descriptions slap you in the face, honest, simple, and elegantly perfect. \\"I have a meanness inside me, real as an organ. Slit me at my belly and it might slide out, meaty and dark, drop on the floor so you could stomp on it.\\" Seriously?  \\"I was not a lovable child, and I'd grown into a deeply unlovable adult. Draw a picture of my soul, and it'd be a scribble with fangs.\\" It took me a good chapter or more before I could stop obsessing over the incredible perfection in the way her words move. I actually had to go back to the beginning of the book because I'd been staring at the words so intently I'd forgotten to back up and take in the story. It was like being in a state of shock. And that's the way I drank up the rest of Dark Places. In a state of wonderfully perfect shock.<br /><br />  Dark Places is the story of Libby Day, the sole survivor of a massacre that wiped out everyone in her family, save herself and her brother Ben, the murderer. The story takes you through 30+ year old Libby's life and her reluctant journey to piece together the truth of that horrible night. At 7, when the massacre occurred, she had testified against her brother and lived her life sure that her finger was pointed in the right direction. But newly broke and without any real skills other than celebrity pity case she's forced to make money the only way she knows how; through the nightmare she survived. As perhaps the only person that can truly get answers from the cast of characters involved in the events of that night, she's tapped by a strange group of murder case fanatics looking for answers to their favorite mystery. Motivated solely by the desperation for money Libby finds herself for the first time in 25 years confronting the reality of that night. As the story unfolds she finds herself drawn deeper and deeper into the people and events of her horrible past than she'd wanted, the uncertainty of her brother's guilt growing inside her. Told through shifting perspectives from Libby, Ben, and Patty (their mother), Dark Places lives up to it's title in all the right ways. As past and present collide, the truth of the Kinnakee Kansas Farmhouse Massacre threatens to destroy it's sole survivor once again.<br /><br />  When I was finally able to get past the shock of Gillian Flynn's surreal prose Dark Places quickly became a story that I couldn't look away from. The train wreck that is Libby Day and her horrible life, was in every way the literary equivalent of it's namesake. Ms. (Mrs.?) Flynn pulls off an incredible feat in making her central character, Libby Day, equal parts wounded and wounding. Well, maybe not equal parts, but certainly a clever balance that makes her angry, bitter, and cold, and yet somehow soft and innocent. The perpetual child -damaged angry and bitter- but still a child. Don't get me wrong, Libby Day is anything but soft and innocent. But somehow Ms. Flynn has crafted her character so that innocence trails her bitter angry nature like a soft, barely visible mist. The story shifts from present to past and back again, from Libby to Ben to their mother Patty, unraveling all the events of their last day. It sounds like a jarring jump, back and forth, but it's pulled off so fluidly that I can't imagine this story being told any other way. Even as the jump is made from Libby's present to Ben or Patty's past, the story builds and moves forward; the present leading us into the past and the past leading us back to the present. Genius. It was like watching a droplet of water roll slowly down the length of a leaf, pause just at the tip, roll over and fall to the ground. This is the way that Dark Places' story moves; fluid and natural. Of course, Dark Places is a story of massacre, brutality, anger, and desperation, so I suppose that droplet of water would more accurately be described as a droplet of blood. Not that there's much of a difference.<br /><br />  I'd say that you'd be missing out if you didn't pick up any one of Gillian Flynn's books, but there's no way to truly say that without sounding like a smitten little fanboy. It's true of course. I am. But that doesn't make her brutally perfect and genius use of the English language any less remarkable. As soon as I was finished with Dark Places, I immediately jumped back into the Kindle store and purchased Sharp Objects and Gone Girl. My only complaint about Gillian Flynn at this point is that she doesn't have a hundred books so that I can just keep reading. I'm not a mystery fan or a thriller fan, or whatever else it is that they call these type of books. Before Dark Places all I did was read classics: Camus, Shakespeare, Dostoyevsky, Dante, Blake, Poe and everything in between and around. My mantra was why bother with modern writers when there are so many incredible classics that I haven't yet read. Gillian Flynn has completely changed my mind. I don't even really know why I took the leap to be honest, but talk about sheer dumb luck. I stumble out of the land of literary gods and land smack dab on Gillian Flynn. When it comes to literature, I get lucky like that sometimes.<br /><br />  As a book in and of itself, Dark Places is a violently smooth tale of a shattered and broken girl piecing together the real story of the horror that's defined and destroyed her life. At it's core it is a mystery wrapped around a massacre, wrapped around a search for the tragic truth of a bloodied and battered life that beganand became very, very wrong. The midwestern setting and it's poverty stricken midwestern characters are living and breathing beings in Gillian Flynn's hands. You are quite literally surrounded and devoured by Dark Places in all the absolutely best ways. But it is words that Gillian Flynn does best. Words and the way they move both within and without the page. Structure, character development, pacing and mystery all move perfectly in Dark Places, and every one of her books; but it's words and the way she moves them that you really have to see for yourself. Opinions are just opinions, and this is just mine, but if you are in any way a fan of literature and all it's moving parts, you will truly be missing out if you don't pick up Dark Places, or for that matter, all of her books.

0375507256|5.0|You open the book to the first page and begin reading a section entitled \\"The Pacific Journal of Adam Ewing\\". Mr. Ewing, you learn, is a public notary from San Francisco. It is the mid-nineteenth century and Mr. Ewing has been sent to the South Sea islands to settle an estate. The journal talks of the islands and then of Mr. Ewing's journey back to San Francisco. After nearly forty pages you turn the page and discover that the next one is blank. The journal has stopped in mid-sentence. You didn't accidentally turn two pages at once, and the your book isn't misprinted -- this is how the novel is written. Unusual.<br /><br />Cloud Atlas: A Novel is comprised of six stories which are linked by themes and, to a lesser extent, characters. The structure is unusual in that a narrative is begun and then dropped in mid-story when another is begun. Five narratives are presented in this fashion and then the sixth narrative is presented as a whole. Then the fifth story is completed, next the fourth is finished, and so on until at the end of the novel we have the conclusion of the first narrative, picked up in mid-sentence. An usual structure for a novel but it works.<br /><br />The novel's structure is an adaptation of a musical structure. The key to understanding this is found in the second narrative which centers on Robert Frobisher, a young twentieth century composer (as well as something of a cad). The phrase \\"cloud atlas\\" appears several times in the novel, but it is central in the Frobisher narrative. \\"Cloud Atlas\\" is the title of Frobisher's magnum opus, a sextet. The appearance of \\"Cloud Atlas\\" here, as a title, and it's centrality to the narrative, points to the relevance of this appearance to the structure of the novel. Frobisher's \\"Cloud Atlas\\" is a sextet, a piece for six instruments, or musically speaking, six voices. Cloud Atlas: A Novel has six narratives, or six voices. The narratives are linked thematically, just as voices in a musical piece are linked. One narrative echos another, or it picks up a point presented in another narrative and develops it.<br /><br />There is one theme which is central to all the narratives in Cloud Atlas: A Novel: power -- power in governments, corporations, social groups and personal relationships. One could say that this is what the book \\"is about\\". Each narrative, and most characters, deal with power. They experience and describe the use, misuse and abuse of power. Some of the characters are misusers and abusers, of course, while others are victims. The theme is presented and then is developed and varied in the several narratives. A very musical presentation of ideas.<br /><br />This description may make Cloud Atlas: A Novel sound like an \\"intellectual novel\\" which, as far as I can tell, means that one is supposed to admire it rather than enjoy it. But this is not the case; Cloud Atlas: A Novel is very enjoyable. Some of the stories pull you in deeply and at places the novel is a real \\"page turner\\". But to enjoy it thusly one must adapt to the unusual structure. More than one person has remarked that it required 50 or so pages before they became truly engaged by the novel. I also found this to be the case. I persevered in the beginning, became hooked, and when finished was very satisfied with the literary journey.

B0078ZYXSA|5.0|To put things in perspective, this is the worst Dexys Midnight Runners album.  It does not compare to the Dexys first two albums, which for this American reviewer, breathed the streets of England into my suburban bedroom and created an energy and musical philosophy that thrilled me like no other band did.  Rush and Iron Maiden were my mainstays back then but for some reason, Dexys first two albums soon became two of the greatest albums I have ever owned.  The difficult third album followed and while the lack of cohesion forces that album to stumble once or twice, Rowland's strong vision and willingness to follow the muse wherever it takes him- mid-song conversations, anyone?- elevates the album to near great heights.<br />  \\"One Day I'm Going To Soar\\" is the worst of these four albums but that being said, it is also a great album. IF it had been released in 1987, it would have sounded like a logical follow-up to \\"Don't Stand Me Down\\".  The music tends to settle into slow jams quite frequently and Kevin's tendency to talk in the middle of a song has not gone away.  The last 27 years never happened, musically, but for a band as confident in its roots as Dexys, it doesn't matter.  The music contains a swagger that announces that Dexys is back and having fun.<br />  \\"Now\\" opens the album low key and a little sultry, setting the stage for the horns and drums to announce themselves with energy and get the song moving along with a little more strut.  \\"Lost\\" and \\"Me\\" are slower numbers, slightly jazzy, highlighting Rowland's vocal chops.  It is during \\"She Got a Wiggle\\" that the band hits its stride.  The groove sounds like a Boz Scaggs outtake or a Steely Dan track from \\"Aja\\"- solid, crisp, smooth, funk.  Rowland's vocals are low key yet suggestive and contrast nicely with the female vocalist haunting the background.<br />  \\"I'm Always Going to Love You\\" is Rowland at his indulgent best, singing his love to a woman who, when she returns the feeling, discovers that Rowland has changed his mind.  He just isn't the type of guy who falls in love.  They argue, playfully, and the music marches on in a slow jam, disco style.  Awesome song.<br />  If you are looking for the Dexys to come up with something new, or if you are simply looking for another \\"Come On Eileen\\", then you may be disappointed.  What they have come up with, however, is an album of confident tracks, performed with style and swagger, played by a band that seems to really enjoy making music. B0071T5PN0|5.0|A bit unconvinced by the single &#34;Tattoo&#34; back in February 2012, I mindlessly dismissed this album because of concerns of how such a long hiatus would impact the chemistry of the (now in their 50s) David Lee Roth and Eddie Van Halen.  I now wish I could go back and buy it on Day 1. What the group has delivered here, despite the near 30 year gap of their partnership, is an unrelenting romp and begs comparison to their earliest sounds. Is it better than Van Halen I or the likes of 1984? No, but in many places, it competes with any of their earliest work and even at times, is indistinguishable from their heyday under the unmistakable showmanship of Roth and the mind-blowing finger tapping of Eddie.  To test this observation, I loaded this album with all of my numerous favorite tracks from 78-84. To my pleasant surprise, many times over I mistook one of Truth's songs for that of their heyday. I've heard that some of the tracks were kicked back up from of their early unrecorded songs. If this is a disappointment to you, don't let it be. Using early rough material as a spring board and also adding new songs is a perfect recipe to confirm the resurrection of this once great and truly influential rock groups.  The intro riffs of She's the Woman, You and Your Blues, Outta Space and Beats Workin' boast classic Van Halen sounds that are immediately recognizable. The one-note bass pulsing and screaming solo work of Big River tempts plenty of headbang impulse. Chinatown and Bullethead are some of the most up-tempo songs I can remember in any of the Van Halen library. The face melting solo and sprawling outtro of As Is (another rapid-fire track) left me with goosebumps.  So if the music is there, it leaves only one question -- how are David Lee Roth's vocals? A subjective questions but for me, above average mostly. Even cynically, they're at least as good as can be hoped for at this point in his career. His lyrics are still largely rooted in free-spirited adolescence, which to me is a relief. While it's true he has lost a bit of range, it was never a detraction for me listening to the album. The screeching choruses of You and Your Blues and Blood & Fire  dispel any worries of Roth's place here.  Perhaps the album's strongest track is Blood and Fire, a rangy number that is rich with tempo change but all sounding seamless. It quite honestly belongs in the discussion of any of Van Halen's finest songs.  Even Tattoo, the fan-debated single that is actually more vintage VH than many people realize, was the first track to get stuck in my head.  In the end, it's apparent that Roth's charisma (albeit corny at times) seems to be the perfect kickstart for Eddie's riffs, the thumps of Alex's drums and the heavy-laden bass brought to life by Eddie's son, Wolfgang Van Halen. Speaking of, I know many fans felt spited by the exclusion of fan-favorite bassist Michael Anthony, but this album only happened largely because of Wolfgang's excitement towards the idea. And trust me, your annoyance will desappear quickly under the thunder of Wolfie's bass -- he BRINGS IT for this record.  Looking at the grand timeline, Eddie may have struggled with Roth's antics from time to time, but this album edges the notion that the two are the best thing that has ever happened to the other.  In the end, A Different KInd of Truth brings out something I've felt was missing from Van Halen since 1984 -- fun. Any Roth or early VH fan should check it out immediately.  Hopefully they'll squeeze out another before their time together is done.

0060558121|3.0|In \\"American Gods\\", the various deities which immigrants to America believed in all manifest themselves as humanoid beings. These \\"old gods\\" struggle to remain relevant and support themselves as the people increasingly lose faith in them and cease their old rituals. \\"New gods\\" - representatives of ideas and things which people worship in some form such as the internet and trains - are created and struggle with the old gods to maintain superiority. The portrayal of these gods as almost human is what sets this book apart from most fantasy. While the gods do have a side to themselves which is more typical of gods in stories, the majority of the story deals with their more human condition and interactions. I was only able to identify a few of the gods included, but that didn't detract from enjoying the book.<br /><br />Shadow is a regular guy just out of prison who gets caught up in the war between the gods. After learning his wife cheated on him and was killed, he doesn't have much left. He's hired by Wednesday, one of the more powerful old gods, as a henchman. Most of the book is told from Shadow's perspective so naturally he's the most important character. Despite this, I didn't feel his character was fleshed out enough. Quite often Shadow just went along with things, a quality which is in line with his wife's assertion at one point that he's not really \\"alive\\". I would've liked to have learned more about why he is the way he is. The little we do learn wasn't enough for me to form a strong opinion about him. As a result, I was able to associate with his character somewhat, but not enough to truly care about what happened to him. This is a problem when he's the main character.<br /><br />Overall I found the story interesting but lacking. Some of the most enjoyable sections were the ones telling some side stories about the origins of some of the gods or their current activities, such as a djinn getting involved with a taxi driver. The main story was compelling at times but also occasionally tedious. It is very descriptive but could've used a little more character development and a lot more emotion.but also occasionally tedious. It is very descriptive but could've used a little more character development and a lot more emotion.

B005LAIHY0|5.0|It appears that many reviewers of Prometheus want to connect it with other films and then complain that too many connections were made to other films.  This reviewer suggests that you should see the film with an open mind and not excessive preconception.  First off, there are absolutely zero tacky scenes in the film.  Director and producer Ridley Scott went to great lengths to avoid rehashing of motifs that have been made trite by all the loosely related films subsequent to Aliens.  By avoiding the gratuitous nature of these films, he has made more subtle suggestions regarding the origins of themes commonly found in the Alien universe.  Additionally, the plot and story are fascinating to consider.  Subjects ranging from the origins of humanity to the relationship between a creator and the created are addressed without providing concrete answers or overwhelming the audience.  And just to note, if you want a film that does all the thinking for you, then you should ask yourself why you want to see this film to begin with.  In other words, Prometheus is not simplistic summer schwag.  Finally, this film has an unbelievably cool sense of style.  It is refreshing to know that there are still directors that can merge kinetics with creative camera angles to construct action sequences that actually contribute to the story.  It is difficult to ask for more from Prometheus than what is already delivered.

Hell the Mexican gang members had more character to them, Salma Hayek balancing being a drug lord and a single mother and Benicio Del Toro being a sadistic henchman with higher aspirations, while John Travolta is hammy as always as a corrupt FBI agent.
